alt="Search" data-reactid="33"/><span data-reactid="34">Search</span></a></div></header><div class="app__content--K0F7y" data-reactid="35"><div class="page" data-reactid="36"><!-- react-empty: 37 --><div class="home__wrapper--j4Xf1 layout__containerFlex--2daqx homeWrapper" data-reactid="38"><div class="features__wrapper--3SCcy" data-reactid="39"><h2 class="features__sectionHeading--1Jz6v typography__sectionHeading--3EBxs" data-reactid="40">Latest Episodes</h2><div class="features__content--1os8m layout__containerFlex--2daqx" data-reactid="41"><div class="features__item--nrcNs features__itemOne--2Rh06" data-reactid="42"><div class="feature__outer--2USsx" data-reactid="43"><div class="feature__wrapper--3b-kJ layout__listViewWrapper--rfblN" data-reactid="44"><div class="feature__content--3dVKZ" data-reactid="45"><h3 class="feature__title--2aRTf typography__listViewItem--3FL-T" data-reactid="46"><a href="/programs/naomi-shihab-nye-your-life-is-a-poem-mar2018/" data-reactid="47">Your Life Is a Poem</a></h3><div data-reactid="48"><ul class="feature__guests--3lbx7" data-reactid="49"><!-- react-text: 50 -->With <!-- /react-text --><li class="feature__guest--1WJK3 typography__listViewMeta--3DBOB" data-reactid="51">Naomi Shihab Nye</li></ul><span class="feature__date--S06e0 typography__listViewDate--gVaZr" data-reactid="52"><time datetime="1521110135000" data-reactid="53">March 15, 2018 | 10:35 AM</time></span><div class="feature__excerpt--2YHov typography__bodyText--8_alT bodyCopy__plainText--3LZKl" data-reactid="54"><p>“When you're in a very quiet place, when you're remembering, when you're savoring an image, when you're allowing your mind calmly to leap from one thought to another, that's a poem.” Naomi Shihab Nye’s poem “Kindness” has traveled around the world. She grew up between Ferguson, Missouri, Ramallah, and Jerusalem. She insists that language must be a way out of cycles of animosity. She’d have us notice “petite discoveries” that embolden us to choose human nourishment over division. “Before you know what kindness really is / you must lose things.”</p>

href="http://civilconversationsproject.org/" to="http://civilconversationsproject.org/" class="textBlocks__wrapper--8INed" data-reactid="428"><h2 class="textBlocks__heading--1jDgZ textBlocks__headingsmall--11Drs" data-reactid="429">The Civil Conversations Project</h2><div class="textBlocks__description--3_kWN typography__bodyText--8_alT" data-reactid="430"><p>The Civil Conversations Project (CCP) is an emergent resource for renewing public discourse and nourishing common life in this century. CCP is a series of radio shows, podcasts, live events, and digital resources that open, inspire, and ground new spaces for conversation and relationship across the differences of our age.</p>

Early in life, he became fascinated with both spirituality and language, a connection fed by his years as an altar boy, and cemented by the nun in his 8th grade year of Catholic school who taught both his English and religion classes. Mallet originally studied journalism in college (he particularly enjoyed copy editing), eventually earning a bachelor's degree in English and then an MFA in fiction.\</p>\n\<p>He spent the bulk of the next 20 years as a college-level writing teacher and writing program administrator in North Carolina, Iowa, and Arizona, where he earned a Ph.D. in rhetoric and composition; he also had a short stint as a technical editor. Mallet's current interests center on the intersection of the personal, the social (especially involving race), and the spiritual, and on writing — its ambiguity and fluidity — as a means to explore that intersection. He believes in writing as a tool for both reflection, disruption, and transformation. Currently at work on both a speculative fiction novel and a long work blending verse, memoir, polemic, and fiction, he writes, runs, and lives in the Twin Cities.\</p>\n","guestDescription":"","name":"Miguel Clark Mallet","email":"mclarkmallet@onbeing.org","publicEmail":"","avatar":"https://api.onbeing.org/wp-content/uploads/2017/01/Miguel-Clark-Mallet-bio.jpg","personalLink":"","archiveLink":"https://api.onbeing.org/author/miguelclarkmallet/","positionTitle":"Columnist","hometown":"","postalZipCode":"","fellowYears":"","socialLinks":{"twitterHandle":"Mar_de_Palabras","instagram":"","facebook":"","linkedin":"","snapchat":"","tumblr":"","medium":""}},"terms":[{"id":2,"name":"Blog","slug":"blog","taxonomy":"category","url":"https://api.onbeing.org/category/blog/","path":"/category/blog/","termId":2,"termGroup":0,"termTaxonomyId":2,"description":"","parent":0,"count":2019,"filter":"raw"},{"id":33,"name":"Community","slug":"community","taxonomy":"post_tag","url":"https://api.onbeing.org/tag/community/","path":"/tag/community/","termId":33,"termGroup":0,"termTaxonomyId":33,"description":"","parent":0,"count":171,"filter":"raw"},{"id":212,"name":"connection","slug":"connection","taxonomy":"post_tag","url":"https://api.onbeing.org/tag/connection/","path":"/tag/connection/","termId":212,"termGroup":0,"termTaxonomyId":212,"description":"","parent":0,"count":29,"filter":"raw"},{"id":5809,"name":"echo discourse","slug":"public-discourse","taxonomy":"post_tag","url":"https://api.onbeing.org/tag/public-discourse/","path":"/tag/public-discourse/","termId":5784,"termGroup":0,"termTaxonomyId":5784,"description":"","parent":0,"count":2,"filter":"raw"},{"id":7,"name":"Race","slug":"race","taxonomy":"post_tag","url":"https://api.onbeing.org/tag/race/","path":"/tag/race/","termId":7,"termGroup":0,"termTaxonomyId":7,"description":"","parent":0,"count":77,"filter":"raw"},{"id":815,"name":"social media","slug":"social-media","taxonomy":"post_tag","url":"https://api.onbeing.org/tag/social-media/","path":"/tag/social-media/","termId":815,"termGroup":0,"termTaxonomyId":815,"description":"","parent":0,"count":8,"filter":"raw"}],"content":"\<blockquote>\<p>\<em>“See man, that’s the difference between us. You think we’re fighting; I think we’re finally talking.”\</em>\</p>\n\<p>— Rod Tidwell, \<a href=\"http://www.imdb.com/title/tt0116695/\">\<em>Jerry Maguire\</em>\</a>\</p>\</blockquote>\n\<p>I still don’t remember how I first heard the news, because tragedies these days arrive less from some network TV bulletin than they leak through social media. You get a text from a friend or loved one: “Have you heard&#8230;?” “You should turn on the TV.” “You should go online.”\</p>\n\<p>But I remember the sledgehammer impact of hearing about the assassinations in Charleston in the summer of 2015.\</p>\n\<p>Trayvon Martin. Eric Garner. Michael Brown. For several years the hashtags multiplied. But the \<a href=\"https://onbeing.org/blog/the-thread-of-violence-to-love-and-learn-from-guns\">killing of nine Black people at Emanuel AME church\</a>, the murders at the hands of a white man whom they had welcomed into their midst to pray with them, punched a hole in my spirit. I wandered into a fog of helpless, \<a href=\"https://onbeing.org/blog/can-we-tell-a-different-story\">hopeless, angry despair\</a> made worse by the silence of the white people I encountered around me. Beyond expressions of short-term shock and sympathy, for them life moved on.\</p>\n\<p>Only when I logged on to Twitter did I find the community of grief that reflected my own reactions.\</p>\n\<p>An introvert by nature, I’ve always been ambivalent about community. My circle of close friendships stays small. Still, I love the hubbub and energy of cities and the chances for interaction and exploration they offer. And since that summer nearly three years ago, I’ve found an invigorating balance between the two in social media.\</p>\n\<p>Of course, I’m not oblivious to Twitter’s perils. The anonymity that helps people share their intimate thoughts, feelings, and struggles invites others to spew cruelty, bigotry, abuse, and threats of violence. Sometimes I’ve been on the receiving end of avalanches of invective when I’ve drawn the ire of one person who had then called on like-minded others to pile on. My favorite single insult is when I was labeled a “virtue-signaling piece of shit.”\</p>\n\<p>I’ve spent more time than I’d like scrolling through my timeline blocking dozens of haters one by one. And my experience pales in comparison to what some have endured — including women, and particularly women of color — that has caused them to leave Twitter altogether. Sexism, racism, homophobia, xenophobia can be found on the site on any given day without much trouble.\</p>\n\<p>Of course, characterizing social media as “dangerous” suggests that for marginalized people life in the offline world is relatively “safe.” In a way, seeing overtly the hatred that people of color have known exists but that generally stays subterranean actually reassured me: It’s \<em>not\</em> my imagination that bigotry still thrives.\</p>\n\<p>Social media also gets criticized as a place where people only \<a href=\"https://onbeing.org/blog/on-echo-chambers-and-everyday-americans\">listen to the like-minded\</a>, where we are less and less exposed to ideas different from those we already hold. I can’t say how generally true that is, but I do know that there are times, particularly for those who have been marginalized, for people of color and oppressed women and the disabled and others who are deemed “different,” when hearing from others who know and see and experience the world as you do can be literally life-saving. We don’t always need someone to agree with us, but we do all need our humanity to be affirmed, and that can be hard to come by for many of us.\</p>\n\<p>This criticism ignores another reality of my Twitter experience because it suggests that people go on the site mainly to talk when I’m drawn by how it enables us to \<em>listen \</em>to one another.\</p>\n\<figure id=\"attachment_152411\" style=\"max-width: 552px\" class=\"wp-caption aligncenter\">\<img data-attachment-id=\"152411\" data-permalink=\"https://onbeing.org/blog/miguel-clark-mallet-city-of-twitter/midsection-of-man-using-mobile-phone/\" class=\"wp-caption-text\">Image By \<a href=\"https://www.gettyimages.com/license/699094073\">Maskot/Getty Images\</a>\</figcaption>\</figure>\n\<p>Nearly everyone I follow on Twitter \<a href=\"https://onbeing.org/blog/grace-in-disagreement-brene-browns-ten-guidelines-for-engaged-feedback\">disagrees with and/or differs from me\</a>. This includes atheists, agnostics, and people with deep religious beliefs; the working class and the wealthy; indigenous people, those of European, Latin, African, Asian descent, and all sorts of mixtures; citizens from the U.S. and those from nations around the world; Republicans, Democrats, libertarians, Greens, socialists, anarchists, and the apolitical; cis and transgender men and women, and those across the spectrum of gender identity and sexuality; those dealing with mental illness or physical disabilities, and those who are not. I read the quarrels among — and within — these groups; I gain a brief window into the experiences of those radically different from me; I learn.\</p>\n\<p>Hearing these multiple voices has also changed the way I think about myself. In various social situations, we highlight aspects of ourselves and downplay others: professional over political, artistic over racial, aggressive over sensitive, playful over serious — or, of course, the opposite depending on where we are. I “am” all of these elements, but no other social space — virtual or otherwise — has felt as accepting of all the aspects of me: the writer, the parent, the partner, the arguer, the thinker, the sports addict, the film fan, the political junkie, the smart aleck, the spiritual seeker.\</p>\n\<p>As I’ve felt freer to be who I am in the moment, exploring my various identities has also released writing voices I had kept within my head. I’ve realized that voices I’d hidden in solitude could actually speak the experiences and feelings of others. And my dialogue between solitude and community has caused me to question how our culture often misrepresents and poisons community.\</p>\n\<p>Too often we define communities in terms of what we share or have in common. But this overlooks community’s other dynamic: disagreement. When we disagree, we create the energy and space for \<a href=\"https://onbeing.org/blog/courtney-martin-the-lie-polite-culture-tells-us-about-conflict\">real dialogue and value exploration\</a>. We learn; we grow.\</p>\n\<p>A family. A city. A country. A corporation. A neighborhood. A religion. A team. No community has a single face or perspective. A community is a collection or those perspectives engaging one another in search for something. What binds a community isn’t agreement but commitment, and the ground of that — or any other — commitment is honesty and good faith.\</p>\n\<p>I believe that what unsettles some about Twitter is the very quality that makes it worthwhile: members’ willingness not only to \<a href=\"https://onbeing.org/blog/miguel-clark-mallet-in-defense-of-backtalk\">challenge one another’s ideas\</a>, beliefs, knowledge, and logic, but to \<em>demand good faith even as they disagree\</em>. This is a challenge not simply to “prove” statements, but to \<em>demonstrate\</em> in the way we converse that we’re trying to get at reality rather than score points.\</p>\n\<p>What some decry as “call-out culture” essentially boils down to a single question: “Are you being honest?” About yourself? About your motives? About your allegiances and values? About what you really believe? About what you really know? About what you claim \<em>not\</em> to know?\</p>\n\<p>I’ve received those challenges. I know how disconcerting they can be. It’s what causes those with privilege to recoil from the critiques of the marginalized. But oppressed and marginalized people have had to live in the chasm between the words and deeds of the powerful. In the context of that history, to say, “I can’t trust your argument until I trust your good will and honesty” is essential. And if I choose to take up that challenge, it forces me into a healthy, reflective solitude, to re-examine myself.\</p>\n\<p>When that happens — however rarely — what looks like fighting ends up really being about talking, the kind of talking that \<a href=\"https://onbeing.org/blog/letting-go-of-fear-to-face-whats-rea\">makes community possible\</a>.\</p>\n\<p>Twitter — and other social media — are communities to me, and, whether we like it or not, they reveal the challenges \<a href=\"https://onbeing.org/programs/danah-boyd-online-reflections-of-our-offline-lives\">our real-world community poses\</a>. We can hide from that challenge, at our peril, or we can embrace it.\</p>\n","excerpt":"Social media gets a bad rap for perpetuating vitriol and echo chambers, but it can also be an platform for our common and civic life — helping us understand people with different backgrounds and opinions, while also allowing us to create communities of our own. Marvel","email":"kmarvel@onbeing.org","publicEmail":"","avatar":"https://api.onbeing.org/wp-content/uploads/2017/07/2017-kate-marvel-bio.jpg","personalLink":"http://www.marvelclimate.com/","archiveLink":"https://api.onbeing.org/author/kate-marvel/","positionTitle":"contributing editor","hometown":"","postalZipCode":"","fellowYears":"","socialLinks":{"twitterHandle":"@DrKateMarvel","instagram":"","facebook":"","linkedin":"","snapchat":"","tumblr":"","medium":""}},"terms":[{"id":2,"name":"Blog","slug":"blog","taxonomy":"category","url":"https://api.onbeing.org/category/blog/","path":"/category/blog/","termId":2,"termGroup":0,"termTaxonomyId":2,"description":"","parent":0,"count":2019,"filter":"raw"},{"id":699,"name":"climate change","slug":"climate-change","taxonomy":"post_tag","url":"https://api.onbeing.org/tag/climate-change/","path":"/tag/climate-change/","termId":699,"termGroup":0,"termTaxonomyId":699,"description":"","parent":0,"count":18,"filter":"raw"},{"id":271,"name":"collective","slug":"collective","taxonomy":"post_tag","url":"https://api.onbeing.org/tag/collective/","path":"/tag/collective/","termId":271,"termGroup":0,"termTaxonomyId":271,"description":"","parent":0,"count":3,"filter":"raw"},{"id":700,"name":"Earth","slug":"earth","taxonomy":"post_tag","url":"https://api.onbeing.org/tag/earth/","path":"/tag/earth/","termId":700,"termGroup":0,"termTaxonomyId":700,"description":"","parent":0,"count":11,"filter":"raw"},{"id":538,"name":"physics","slug":"physics","taxonomy":"post_tag","url":"https://api.onbeing.org/tag/physics/","path":"/tag/physics/","termId":538,"termGroup":0,"termTaxonomyId":538,"description":"","parent":0,"count":30,"filter":"raw"},{"id":285,"name":"science","slug":"science","taxonomy":"post_tag","url":"https://api.onbeing.org/tag/science/","path":"/tag/science/","termId":285,"termGroup":0,"termTaxonomyId":285,"description":"","parent":0,"count":63,"filter":"raw"}],"content":"\<p class=\"p1\">As a climate scientist, I am often asked to talk about hope. Particularly in the current political climate, audiences want to be told that everything will be all right in the end. And, unfortunately, I have a deep-seated need to be liked and a natural tendency to optimism that leads me to accept more speaking invitations than is good for me. Climate change is bleak, the organizers always say. Tell us a happy story. Give us hope. The problem is, I don’t have any.\</p>\n\<p class=\"p1\">I used to believe there was \<a href=\"https://onbeing.org/blog/rebecca-delker-science-is-far-too-often-communicated-as-a-one-sided-conversation\">hope in science\</a>. The fact that we know anything at all is a miracle. For some reason, the whole world is hung on a skeleton made of physics. I found comfort in this structure, in the knowledge that buried under layers of greenery and dirt lies something universal. It is something to know how to cut away the flesh of existence and see the clean white bones underneath. All of us obey the same laws, whether we know them or not.\</p>\n\<p class=\"p1\">Look closely, however, and the structure of physics dissolves into uncertainty. We live in a statistical world, in a limit where we experience only one of many possible outcomes. Our clumsy senses perceive only gross aggregates, blind to the roiling chaos underneath. We are limited in our ability to see the underlying stimuli that, en masse, create an event. Temperature, for example, is a state created by the random motions of millions of tiny molecules. We feel heat or cold, not the motion of any individual molecule. When something is heated up, its tiny constituent parts move faster, increasing its internal energy. They do not move at the same speed; some are quick, others slow. But there are billions of them, and in the aggregate their speed dictates their temperature.\</p>\n\<p class=\"p1\">The internal energy of molecule motion is turned outward in the form of electromagnetic radiation. Light comes in different flavors. The stuff we see occupies only a tiny portion of a vast electromagnetic spectrum. What we see occupies a tiny portion of a vast electromagnetic spectrum. Light is a wave, of sorts, and the distance between its peaks and troughs determines the energy it carries. Cold, low-energy objects emit stretched waves with long, lazy intervals between peaks. Hot objects radiate at shorter wavelengths.\</p>\n\<p class=\"p1\">To have a temperature is to shed light into your surroundings. You have one. The light you give off is invisible to the naked eye. You are shining all the same, incandescent with the power of a hundred-watt bulb. The planet on which you live is illuminated by the visible light of the sun and radiates infrared light to the blackness of space. There is nothing that does not have a temperature. Cold space itself is illuminated by the afterglow of the Big Bang. Even black holes radiate, lit by the strangeness of quantum mechanics. There is nowhere from which light cannot escape.\</p>\n\<p class=\"p1\">The same laws that flood the world with light dictate the behavior of a carbon dioxide molecule in the atmosphere. CO\<sub>2\</sub> is transparent to the Sun’s rays. But the planet’s infrared outflow hits a molecule in just such as way as to set it in motion. Carbon dioxide dances when hit by a quantum of such light, arresting the light on its path to space. When the dance stops, the quantum is released back to the atmosphere from which it came. No one feels the consequences of this individual catch-and-release, but the net result of many little dances is an increase in the temperature of the planet. More CO\<sub>2\</sub> molecules mean a warmer atmosphere and a warmer planet. Warm seas fuel hurricanes, warm air bloats with water vapor, the rising sea encroaches on the land. The consequences of tiny random acts echo throughout the world.\</p>\n\<p class=\"p1\">I understand the physical world because, at some level, I understand the behavior of every small thing. I know how to assemble a coarse aggregate from the sum of multiple tiny motions. Individual molecules, water droplets, parcels of air, quanta of light: their random movements merge to yield a predictable and understandable whole. But physics is unable to explain the whole of the world in which I live. The planet teems with other people: seven billion fellow damaged creatures. We come together and break apart, seldom adding up to an coherent, predictable whole.\</p>\n\<p class=\"p1\">I have lived a fortunate, charmed, loved life. This means I have infinite, gullible faith in the goodness of the individual. But I have none whatsoever in the collective. How else can it be that the \<a href=\"https://onbeing.org/programs/bill-mckibben-the-moral-math-of-climate-change\">sum total\</a> of so many tiny acts of kindness is a world incapable of stopping something so eminently stoppable? California burns. Islands and coastlines are smashed by hurricanes. At night the stars are washed out by city lights and the world is illuminated by the flickering ugliness of reality television. We burn coal and oil and gas, heedless of the consequences.\</p>\n\<p class=\"p1\">Our laws are changeable and shifting; the laws of physics are fixed. Change is already underway; individual worries and sacrifices have not slowed it. Hope is a creature of privilege: we know that things will be lost, but it is comforting to believe that others will bear the brunt of it.\</p>\n\<p class=\"p1\">We are the lucky ones who suffer little tragedies unmoored from the brutality of history. Our loved ones are taken from us one by one through accident or illness, not wholesale by war or natural disaster. But the scale of climate change engulfs even the most fortunate. There is now no weather we haven’t touched, no wilderness immune from our encroaching pressure. The world we once knew is never coming back.\</p>\n\<p class=\"p1\">I have no hope that these changes can be reversed. We are inevitably \<a href=\"https://onbeing.org/blog/kate-marvel-we-should-never-have-called-it-earth\">sending our children\</a> to live on an unfamiliar planet. But the opposite of hope is not despair. It is grief. Even while resolving to limit the damage, we can mourn. And here, the sheer scale of the problem provides a perverse comfort: we are in this together. The swiftness of the change, its scale and inevitability, binds us into one, broken hearts trapped together under a warming atmosphere.\</p>\n\<p class=\"p1\">We need courage, not hope. Grief, after all, is the cost of being alive. We are all fated to live lives shot through with sadness, and are not worth less for it. Courage is the resolve to do well without the assurance of a happy ending. Little molecules, random in their movement, add together to a coherent whole. Little lives do not. But \<a href=\"https://onbeing.org/blog/beyond-rio-and-halki-climate-change-may-rest-in-engaging-hearts-and-organizing-hands\">here we are\</a>, together on a planet radiating ever more into space where there is no darkness, only light we cannot see.\</p>\n","excerpt":"Where do we go when we are beyond hope? A climate scientist reflects on the courage we need to accept that we must continue to pursue solutions, even in the face of failure.","url":"https://onbeing.org/blog/kate-marvel-we-need-courage-not-hope-to-face-climate-change/","path":"/blog/kate-marvel-we-need-courage-not-hope-to-face-climate-change/","metadata":{"type":"post","featuredMedia":"https://i1.wp.com/api.onbeing.org/wp-content/uploads/2018/02/Marvel-030118-A.jpg?fit=4000%2C6016&ssl=1","imageCredit":"","imageCreditLink":"","episode":{"title":"","audioUrl":"","audioUrlUnedited":"","audioUrlUneditedFile":"","audioUrlEmbed":"","description":"","shortDescription":"","guests":[]}},"episodeMeta":{"releaseDate":"","title":"","strapline":"","shortDescription":"","description":"","guests":"","audioUrl":""},"type":"post","featuredMedia":"https://i1.wp.com/api.onbeing.org/wp-content/uploads/2018/02/Marvel-030118-A.jpg?fit=4000%2C6016&ssl=1"},{"id":89271,"date":"2018-02-26T16:38:51+00:00","modifiedDate":"2018-02-26T16:41:21+00:00","slug":"kao-kalia-yang-a-refugee-woman-on-antidepressants","title":"A Refugee Woman on Antidepressants","author":{"id":"1003","slug":"kao-kalia-yang","description":"\<p>is a Hmong-American teacher, public speaker, and writer. She is the author of the award-winning book \<a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Latehomecomer-Hmong-Family-Memoir/dp/1566892082/\">\<em>The Latehomecomer: A Hmong Family Memoir\</em>\</a> and \<a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Song-Poet-Memoir-My-Father/dp/125013188X\">\<em>The Song Poet\</em>\</a>, nominated for a National Book Critics Circle Award in 2017. She is a graduate of Carleton College and Columbia University’s School of the Arts. Kao Kalia lives in Minneapolis, Minnesota with her family.\</p>\n","guestDescription":"","name":"Kao Kalia Yang","email":"kyang@onbeing.org","publicEmail":"","avatar":"https://api.onbeing.org/wp-content/uploads/2017/05/YNH7Yori.jpg","personalLink":"http://www.kaokaliayang.com/","archiveLink":"https://api.onbeing.org/author/kao-kalia-yang/","positionTitle":"columnist","hometown":"","postalZipCode":"","fellowYears":"","socialLinks":{"twitterHandle":"KaoKaliaYang","instagram":"","facebook":"","linkedin":"","snapchat":"","tumblr":"","medium":""}},"terms":[{"id":2,"name":"Blog","slug":"blog","taxonomy":"category","url":"https://api.onbeing.org/category/blog/","path":"/category/blog/","termId":2,"termGroup":0,"termTaxonomyId":2,"description":"","parent":0,"count":2019,"filter":"raw"},{"id":231,"name":"depression","slug":"depression","taxonomy":"post_tag","url":"https://api.onbeing.org/tag/depression/","path":"/tag/depression/","termId":231,"termGroup":0,"termTaxonomyId":231,"description":"","parent":0,"count":32,"filter":"raw"},{"id":5535,"name":"Hmong","slug":"hmong","taxonomy":"post_tag","url":"https://api.onbeing.org/tag/hmong/","path":"/tag/hmong/","termId":5535,"termGroup":0,"termTaxonomyId":5535,"description":"","parent":0,"count":5,"filter":"raw"},{"id":900,"name":"loneliness","slug":"loneliness","taxonomy":"post_tag","url":"https://api.onbeing.org/tag/loneliness/","path":"/tag/loneliness/","termId":900,"termGroup":0,"termTaxonomyId":900,"description":"","parent":0,"count":17,"filter":"raw"},{"id":5477,"name":"Migration","slug":"migration","taxonomy":"post_tag","url":"https://api.onbeing.org/tag/migration/","path":"/tag/migration/","termId":5477,"termGroup":0,"termTaxonomyId":5477,"description":"","parent":0,"count":2,"filter":"raw"},{"id":326,"name":"refugee","slug":"refugee","taxonomy":"post_tag","url":"https://api.onbeing.org/tag/refugee/","path":"/tag/refugee/","termId":326,"termGroup":0,"termTaxonomyId":326,"description":"","parent":0,"count":6,"filter":"raw"}],"content":"\<p>Kho Kho Siab is loneliness in the Hmong language. Kho is to fix. Siab is to heart. Fix the heart. Please.\</p>\n\<p>The woman laid on the sofa, her head on its leathery arm, her cell phone in her hand. She’d put on weight. With each breath she took, her stomach rose, a soft mound of flesh beneath the polyester shirt she wore. She needed a haircut. Her black hair kept short had grown long, its strands covering most of her eyes like a teenage boy’s. Her hair hadn’t been this greasy since the days when she worked the night shift and then spent the rest of her time caring for her young children. The time she could use to wash her hair, she used to sleep instead.\</p>\n\<p>She looked sluggish, sagging flesh and clothing, sitting there on the sofa, her iPhone in her hands.\</p>\n\<p>The woman spent all her time looking at flowers on the Internet. She knew how to find and save the photos, flowers everywhere, from around the world, the tulip fields of Holland, the orchids of Thailand, the beautiful Buddhist lotus ponds, the purple heathers abloom on the English moors. Once she saved the photos, she spent hours editing them, changing the filters and cropping the images. She said she had over 11,000 pictures on her phone.\</p>\n\<p>Sometimes, she was afraid. She said that black things moved \<a href=\"https://onbeing.org/blog/kao-kalia-yang-two-hauntings\">across the living room floor\</a>, the size of cats. Her eyes frantic, she made no sounds. Instead, she froze in place. The only movement her eyes helplessly searching for an exit from her body.\</p>\n\<p>She had vivid dreams full of handsome men who came into her life on horses. They all wanted to marry her and take her away. In the dreams, she wanted to go with them, except she couldn’t quite lift herself off the ground and onto their high horses. Her body was heavy in the dreams, anchored firmly to the earth. Sometimes, she woke up reaching for the handsome men only to feel the empty, lonely air of their absence.\</p>\n\<p>She couldn’t sleep at night without her drugs. Even with them, in the day she was tired, drowsy, had no heart for anything or anyone, she said.\</p>\n\<p>Sometimes, she \<a href=\"https://onbeing.org/blog/kao-kalia-yang-when-relatives-die-they-become-ancestors\">cried for her dead father\</a>. It’d been nearly half a century since his death but to her it was fresh as the morning. She said she knew where he was, always at the edge of the horizon. There he waited for her with wings like the birds. He sent messages to her. He offered a promise to lift her up high so they could soar through the skies together. From there, he told her, she wouldn’t have to miss her kids because she could still see them on earth.\</p>\n\<p>Most of the time, though, she spent her days on the sofa looking at her phone.\</p>\n\<p>She did not have the energy to cook or clean, two things she’d always attended to as a matter of life and death for her children.\</p>\n\<p>In the past, no matter how little sleep she was functioning on, she made traditional Hmong meals with no store-bought sauces and little fat for her family. She boiled fish with lemongrass, tomatoes, green onions, culantro, mint, basil, red hot chili peppers, cilantro, a touch of MSG, and then salt to taste. She made bowls of rice in tofu and chicken soup. Her children loved her mung bean thread soup with minced pork and green onions and cilantro, black pepper galore. As they ate, she stood at the sink waiting to wash each plate, bowl, spoon, and fork.\</p>\n\<p>Now, she herself was never hungry and her children’s hunger held no more power to get her up.\</p>\n\<p>All her life, \<a href=\"https://onbeing.org/blog/kao-kalia-yang-my-father-is-not-a-powerful-man-lessons-from-my-refugee-father/\">no matter where they were\</a> — in the camps of Thailand or the housing projects of Minnesota — her and her husband’s bedroom was a place where the bed was always made, the floor clean, a space where clothes were folded in baskets, hung in closets, stored in drawers. No matter the mess beyond its doors, the chaos of the unsteady world was never allowed to enter. Now, she’d place a blanket on the floor. The mountain of clean clothes that needed to be folded and put away was nearly as high as their rumpled bed. Now, the dust of their lives had settled onto her body and had grown so thick she could barely move.\</p>\n\<p>Her husband, too used to the woman from the past, perhaps hoping that she would return in light of the current conditions of their room, did not pull the covers over the sheets in the mornings or sit to fold the clothes, so the pile grew higher and higher.\</p>\n\<p>At the doctor’s offices, the nurses gave her evaluations. Worksheets for her to circle her emotions on: how strong they were, how much pain she was in. (On the question of pain — it was always physical, never otherwise.)\</p>\n\<p>Each time she said, “the same,” the doctors increased the dosage of her prescriptions. When one prescription did not work, the doctors shifted to another. The woman was put on Wellbutrin, Prozac, Zoloft, Paxil, Effexor, Cymbalta, and more.\</p>\n\<p>One pill made her believe that she was on the cusp of death. Her heart would start racing. She grew short of breath. She spoke her death wishes: Keep things simple at the funeral; I don’t want anything fancy; follow the most rudimentary details of tradition.\</p>\n\<p>At the doctor’s office, on the suicide question, she shook her head adamantly, and said, “My biggest fear is that I’ll die and leave my children behind. I would never kill myself.”\</p>\n\<p>She wept.\</p>\n\<p>The doctors asked why she was so sad.\</p>\n\<p>She answered, “I don’t know what to do with myself. I am out of work. I’m too young to be useless. My children need a mother who can take care of them.”\</p>\n\<p>The doctors wanted her to go into the world. She didn’t drive. She didn’t speak good English. Her hands hurt. Her feet hurt. Her neck hurt. She was falling apart. The doctors said they could not change the conditions of her life, that the only thing they could do was change the conditions of her head. No one knew what to do with her heart.\</p>\n\<p>Her fiery heart. The heart of the little girl, the only one in her village to go to school, to race in front of the boys in the classroom. The heart of the young woman who’d chosen love in a war full of death and despair, made a call to build a life from a world falling apart. The heart of the mother who would place \<a href=\"https://onbeing.org/blog/kao-kalia-yang-your-threads-have-come-undone-a-letter-to-a-grieving-husband\">each of her children, alive and dead\</a>, before herself with no misgivings or regrets. The heart of a wife who did everything hard so her husband, in a world that had given up on him, could not give up on her, thus their life together. Now, her heart was hurt.\</p>\n\<p>The pills made the woman so tired she could not fret about going into the world. They numbed her body so she could not feel the pain in her hands, her feet, her neck. They glued her together in a bubble of despair. The doctors could not change the conditions of the woman’s life, but they made it so that she could not interact with them or us.\</p>\n\<p>Each day, the woman floated further and further away, all by herself, in a bubble full of flowering images, framed and filtered. One day, the bubble rose high, a brisk wind blew, and it lifted far into the clouds of another world. The woman became a shining particle in the wide openness of everything.\</p>\n","excerpt":"The doctors said they could not change the conditions of her life, that the only thing they could do was change the conditions of her head. No one knew what to do with her heart.","url":"https://onbeing.org/blog/kao-kalia-yang-a-refugee-woman-on-antidepressants/","path":"/blog/kao-kalia-yang-a-refugee-woman-on-antidepressants/","metadata":{"type":"post","featuredMedia":"https://i1.wp.com/api.onbeing.org/wp-content/uploads/2018/02/GettyImages-51216253.jpg?fit=3000%2C2002&ssl=1","imageCredit":"","imageCreditLink":"","episode":{"title":"","audioUrl":"","audioUrlUnedited":"","audioUrlUneditedFile":"","audioUrlEmbed":"","description":"","shortDescription":"","guests":[]}},"episodeMeta":{"releaseDate":"","title":"","strapline":"","shortDescription":"","description":"","guests":"","audioUrl":""},"type":"post","featuredMedia":"https://i1.wp.com/api.onbeing.org/wp-content/uploads/2018/02/GettyImages-51216253.jpg?fit=3000%2C2002&ssl=1"}],"quote":"\<p>The human soul doesn’t want to be advised or fixed or saved. It simply wants to be witnessed — to be seen, heard and companioned exactly as it is.\</p>\n","citation":"Parker Palmer","ctaLabel":"Read more","ctaLink":"https://onbeing.org/blog/the-gift-of-presence-the-perils-of-advice/"},{"id":"e571fe33c1aab77e758417084995c3bb","type":"collections","title":"Favorite Collections","collectionIds":[39157,43301],"collections":[{"heading":"Public Theology Reimagined","items":[{"id":28707,"date":"2017-11-06T16:30:50+00:00","modifiedDate":"2017-11-06T18:06:59+00:00","slug":"richard-rohr-utterly-humbled-by-mystery","title":"Utterly Humbled by Mystery","author":{"id":"972","slug":"richard-rohr","description":"\<p>is founder of the Center for Action and Contemplation in Albuquerque, New Mexico. I believe in mystery and multiplicity. To religious believers this may sound almost pagan. But I don&#8217;t think so. My very belief and experience of a loving and endlessly creative God has led me to trust in both.\</p>\n\<p>I&#8217;ve had the good fortune of teaching and preaching across much of the globe, while also struggling to \<a href=\"https://onbeing.org/programs/richard-rohr-living-in-deep-time-apr2017/\">make sense of my experience in my own tiny world\</a>. This life journey has led me to love mystery and not feel the need to change it or make it un-mysterious. This has put me at odds with many other believers I know who seem to need explanations for everything.\</p>\n\<p>Religious belief has made me comfortable with ambiguity. &#8220;Hints and guesses,&#8221; \<a href=\"https://brianzahnd.com/2011/10/hints-and-guesses/\">as T.S. Eliot would say\</a>. I often spend the season of Lent in a hermitage, where I live alone for the whole 40 days. The more I am alone with the Alone, the more I surrender to ambivalence, to happy contradictions and seeming inconsistencies in myself and almost everything else, including God. Paradoxes don&#8217;t scare me anymore.\</p>\n\<p>When I was young, I couldn&#8217;t tolerate such ambiguity. My education had trained me to have a lust for answers and explanations. Now, at age 63, \<a href=\"https://onbeing.org/blog/choosing-to-marvel-at-mystery/\">it&#8217;s all quite different\</a>. I no longer believe this is a quid pro quo universe — I&#8217;ve counseled too many prisoners, worked with too many failed marriages, faced my own dilemmas too many times and been loved gratuitously after too many failures.\</p>\n\<p>Whenever I think there&#8217;s a perfect pattern, further reading and study reveal an exception. Whenever I want to say &#8220;only&#8221; or &#8220;always,&#8221; someone or something proves me wrong. My scientist friends have come up with things like &#8220;principles of uncertainty&#8221; and dark holes. They&#8217;re willing \<a href=\"https://onbeing.org/programs/daniel-kahneman-why-we-contradict-ourselves-and-confound-each-other-oct2017/\">to live inside imagined hypotheses and theories\</a>. But many religious folks insist on \<em>answers\</em> that are \<em>always\</em> true. We love closure, resolution and, clarity, while thinking that we are people of &#8220;faith&#8221;! How strange that the very word &#8220;faith&#8221; has come to mean its exact opposite.\</p>\n\<p>People who have really met the Holy are always humble. It&#8217;s the people who don&#8217;t know who usually pretend that they do. People who&#8217;ve had any genuine spiritual experience always know they \<em>don&#8217;t know\</em>. They are utterly humbled before mystery. They are in awe before the abyss of it all, in wonder at eternity and depth, and a Love, which is incomprehensible to the mind. It is a litmus test for authentic God experience, and is — quite sadly — absent from much of our religious conversation today. My belief and comfort is in the depths of Mystery, which should be the very task of religion.\</p>\n\<p>\<iframe width=\"500\" height=\"400\" scrolling=\"no\" frameborder=\"no\" src=\"https://w.soundcloud.com/player/?visual=true&#038;url=https%3A%2F%2Fapi.soundcloud.com%2Ftracks%2F317541621&#038;show_artwork=true&#038;maxwidth=500&#038;maxheight=750&#038;dnt=1\">\</iframe>\</p>\n\<hr />\n\<p>\<em>This essay was originally published by \<a href=\"http://www.npr.org/templates/story/story.php?storyId=6631954\">NPR&#8217;s This I Believe series\</a>. It is reprinted here with permission.\</em>\</p>\n","excerpt":"We crave the closure of explanations and answers, but what if we were enlivened by the questions themselves? On the evolution of his own faith — from a hunger for certainty to awe at the ineffable.","url":"https://onbeing.org/blog/richard-rohr-utterly-humbled-by-mystery/","path":"/blog/richard-rohr-utterly-humbled-by-mystery/","metadata":{"type":"post","featuredMedia":"https://i0.wp.com/api.onbeing.org/wp-content/uploads/2017/05/nicholas-green-377162.jpg?fit=5210%2C3589&ssl=1","imageCredit":"","imageCreditLink":"","episode":{"title":"","audioUrl":"","audioUrlUnedited":"","audioUrlUneditedFile":"","audioUrlEmbed":"","description":"","shortDescription":"","guests":[]}},"episodeMeta":{"releaseDate":"","title":"","strapline":"","shortDescription":"","description":"","guests":"","audioUrl":""},"type":"post","featuredMedia":"https://i0.wp.com/api.onbeing.org/wp-content/uploads/2017/05/nicholas-green-377162.jpg?fit=5210%2C3589&ssl=1"},{"id":35349,"date":"2017-10-23T17:02:46+00:00","modifiedDate":"2017-10-23T17:02:46+00:00","slug":"julian-deshazier-hip-hop-job-and-the-black-struggle-for-being","title":"Hip-Hop, Job, and the Black Struggle for Being","author":{"id":"1061","slug":"julian_deshazier","description":"\<p>is pastor of University Church in Chicago. He is also an award-winning hip hop musician and songwriter, known to many as “\<a title=\"J.Kwest\" href=\"http://www.jkwest.com\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\">J.Kwest\</a>.” He is a graduate of Morehouse College and the University of Chicago Divinity School. In 2015, he won an EMMY Award for his role in the short film, Strange Fruit.\</p>\n","guestDescription":"","name":"Julian DeShazier","email":"JulianDeShazier@onbeing.org","publicEmail":"","avatar":"https://api.onbeing.org/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/JKHeadshot_0.jpg","personalLink":"http://universitychurchchicago.org/team/rev-julian-deshazier-senior-pastor/","archiveLink":"https://api.onbeing.org/author/julian_deshazier/","positionTitle":"guest contributor","hometown":"","postalZipCode":"","fellowYears":"","socialLinks":{"twitterHandle":"@PureKwest","instagram":"","facebook":"","linkedin":"","snapchat":"","tumblr":"","medium":""}},"terms":[{"id":2,"name":"Blog","slug":"blog","taxonomy":"category","url":"https://api.onbeing.org/category/blog/","path":"/category/blog/","termId":2,"termGroup":0,"termTaxonomyId":2,"description":"","parent":0,"count":2019,"filter":"raw"},{"id":5433,"name":"Public Theology Reimagined","slug":"public-theology-reimagined-blog","taxonomy":"category","url":"https://api.onbeing.org/category/public-theology-reimagined-blog/","path":"/category/public-theology-reimagined-blog/","termId":5433,"termGroup":0,"termTaxonomyId":5433,"description":"","parent":0,"count":52,"filter":"raw"},{"id":957,"name":"African American","slug":"african-american","taxonomy":"post_tag","url":"https://api.onbeing.org/tag/african-american/","path":"/tag/african-american/","termId":957,"termGroup":0,"termTaxonomyId":957,"description":"","parent":0,"count":25,"filter":"raw"},{"id":98,"name":"Bible","slug":"bible","taxonomy":"post_tag","url":"https://api.onbeing.org/tag/bible/","path":"/tag/bible/","termId":98,"termGroup":0,"termTaxonomyId":98,"description":"","parent":0,"count":30,"filter":"raw"},{"id":985,"name":"black","slug":"black","taxonomy":"post_tag","url":"https://api.onbeing.org/tag/black/","path":"/tag/black/","termId":985,"termGroup":0,"termTaxonomyId":985,"description":"","parent":0,"count":16,"filter":"raw"},{"id":2900,"name":"hip-hop","slug":"hip-hop-2","taxonomy":"post_tag","url":"https://api.onbeing.org/tag/hip-hop-2/","path":"/tag/hip-hop-2/","termId":2900,"termGroup":0,"termTaxonomyId":2900,"description":"","parent":0,"count":8,"filter":"raw"},{"id":1944,"name":"Job","slug":"job","taxonomy":"post_tag","url":"https://api.onbeing.org/tag/job/","path":"/tag/job/","termId":1944,"termGroup":0,"termTaxonomyId":1944,"description":"","parent":0,"count":6,"filter":"raw"},{"id":46,"name":"Music","slug":"music","taxonomy":"post_tag","url":"https://api.onbeing.org/tag/music/","path":"/tag/music/","termId":46,"termGroup":0,"termTaxonomyId":46,"description":"","parent":0,"count":116,"filter":"raw"},{"id":348,"name":"pop culture","slug":"pop-culture","taxonomy":"post_tag","url":"https://api.onbeing.org/tag/pop-culture/","path":"/tag/pop-culture/","termId":348,"termGroup":0,"termTaxonomyId":348,"description":"","parent":0,"count":30,"filter":"raw"},{"id":607,"name":"rap","slug":"rap","taxonomy":"post_tag","url":"https://api.onbeing.org/tag/rap/","path":"/tag/rap/","termId":607,"termGroup":0,"termTaxonomyId":607,"description":"","parent":0,"count":10,"filter":"raw"}],"content":"\<p>The opening of \<a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=mFSlhTKmEUI\">De La Soul’s “Intro”\</a> (from the \<em>Stakes Is High\</em> album) is an expertly mixed chorus of four voices saying these six words…\</p>\n\<blockquote>\<p>\<em>When I first heard “Criminal Minded&#8230;”\</em>\</p>\</blockquote>\n\<p>&#8230;which refer to Boogie Down Productions’ 1987 album of that name, one of the most acclaimed albums in hip-hop history. De La Soul’s sentiment is clear and can easily be translated to “When I first heard \<em>that album that changed my life\</em>,” be it from \<a href=\"https://www.allmusic.com/artist/boogie-down-productions-mn0000772127/biography\">BDP\</a> or \<a href=\"https://www.biography.com/people/black-thought-5182017\">Black Thought\</a> or \<a href=\"https://onbeing.org/blog/bob-dylan-old-testament-language-beat-poetics-and-a-theology-of-service/\">Bob Dylan\</a>: the moment the listener hears an album that is both talking \<em>to\</em> and \<em>for\</em> them, bearing witness to a reality and helping to create a better reality. BDP and its lead rapper KRS-One became entertainers, journalists, and prophets to a South Bronx, N.Y., context full of poverty, drugs, and pop music — whether disco or rock — that was served to them but not made by them. As invisible as the politics of the day made them, hip-hop represented the soundtrack of resistance.\</p>\n\<p>The notion of creating music as a way of creating or articulating reality has its roots in other genres. James Cone reminds us that \<a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Spirituals-Blues-Interpretation-James-Cone/dp/0883448432\">blues music was created in the midst of the black struggle for being\</a> in another era, King David of Israel becomes a brilliant psalmist (that is “songwriter”) in the midst of deep pain, and \<a href=\"https://sites.google.com/site/hiphopculturestudies210/the-team\">Samuel Livingston\</a> traces humankind’s first songs to the African concept of \<em>neferu\</em> (cultural manifestations of functional beauty). In other words, music has always had a purpose before it had an industry, and its economy was purely social, made up of those who would listen, identify, and be identified by those artists.\</p>\n\<blockquote>\<p>\<em>When I first heard “Criminal Minded”\</em>\<br />\n\<em>When I first heard 2Pac’s “All Eyez on Me” \</em>\<br />\n\<em>When I first heard Common’s “One Day It’ll All Make Sense”\</em>\<br />\n\<em>…It changed my world\</em>\<br />\n\<em>…Because I heard myself, for the first time\</em>\</p>\</blockquote>\n\<p>Rap is scary to some because it is loud, which is entirely the point. It is a response with deep intention toward the systemic silencing by privileged whites, the wealthy, and the ignorant men who hold the center at this particular moment in time. It is the first bell that rings after the death around us young black Chicagoans has silenced us.\</p>\n\<p>It is the cathartic response of Job after being stunned over and over, pathologically pummeled to the brink of nonbeing, and his first words — “Let the day perish on which I was born… let that day be darkness!” — which begin to reaffirm and reconstruct his being. His words are harsh and explicit and feel grossly emotional; in our context they would seem anti-intellectual when in fact they are \<em>super\</em>-rational, transcending intellect.\</p>\n\<p>In 1988 and today, N.W.A.’s “Fuck Tha Police” is shocking and controversial — “uncalled for” by most tastes — until you hear the songwriters recall their inspiration, being pulled over in Los Angeles, handcuffed and forced to lie on the ground \<a href=\"https://onbeing.org/blog/miguel-clark-mallet-carrying-the-weight-of-how-the-white-world-imagines-you/\">for being one thing: black\</a>. Was N.W.A. having a Job moment, or was Job having one of the first hip-hop moments? Either way, both texts comprise wisdom, both utterances remain necessary.\</p>\n\<p>If you want to understand the violence epidemic in Chicago, listen to the “drill” music of the shooters. You will hear the struggle for being that too often describes and destroys. If you want to understand the beautiful complexity of our youth, listen to \<a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=DVkkYlQNmbc\">Chance the Rapper’s “No Problem,”\</a> where he talks about “scoopin’ all the blessings out my lap” and by the end of the same verse brusquely reminds us that his “shooters come for free.” His message is clear: He’s keeping his, by any means necessary.\</p>\n\<p>What he’s really saying — perhaps in every verse Chance has ever written — is that his being matters. His album \<a href=\"https://soundcloud.com/chancetherapper/sets/coloring-book\">\<em>Coloring Book\</em>\</a>, along with \<a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Criminal-Minded-Boogie-Down-Productions/dp/B000005X5Y\">BDP’s \<em>Criminal Minded\</em>\</a>, expresses the “courage to be” without probably ever hearing (and certainly never caring about) the name \<a href=\"https://www.britannica.com/biography/Paul-Tillich\">Paul Tillich\</a>. These are theological projects as much as they are musical ones, and hip-hop has a way of reminding us that the separation of the head and heart is mostly an academic and superficial one. Job should have written an album; maybe Notorious B.I.G. was reading the famous text when he settled on a name for his first album, \<em>Ready to Die\</em>.\</p>\n\<p>Growing up on the Southside of Chicago in rap’s “Golden Era,” I had no need for church; I had already found \<a href=\"https://onbeing.org/programs/cloud-cult-music-is-medicine-aug2017/\">an adequate object of worship in the music\</a> that blared through my cassette player — music from Christians and Muslims and \<a href=\"http://www.npr.org/templates/story/story.php?storyId=5614846\">Five-Percenters\</a> and Black Hebrew Israelites — music that was loud and confident and confrontational, \<em>explicit\</em> in every sense of the word. For all I had seen and endured, it needed to be.\</p>\n\<p>We — that is, most young, black boys — take our cues from rappers, perhaps to a fault, but at least they show better journalistic integrity in accurately describing reality than most news outlets. This trust in \<em>entertainer as journalist\</em> evolves rappers into a greater role: the poets who shape culture, the poetry whose purpose is to create a new reality. You hear rappers who don’t understand this and use their microphone to spread a dangerous gospel of misogynist and capitalist urges — no different from some pulpits. But you also hear the proclamation and affirmation of the #BlackLivesMatter movement, its anger and its spirit engorged as a people fight to be made visible. In the rhythm and lineage you see Africa. It is \<em>neferu. \</em>It is the catharsis of Job. And for those hearing it for the first time…\</p>\n\<blockquote>\<p>\<em>When I first heard “Criminal Minded”\</em>\<br />\n\<em>When I first read Job\</em>\<br />\n\<em>When I saw myself\</em>\</p>\</blockquote>\n\<p>…it is like coming alive again. It is that Resurrection that many classically trained theologians spend too many words describing. It is a hip-hop moment.\</p>\n\<hr />\n\<div>\<em>This essay originally appeared in \<span class=\"il\">Sightings\</span>, a publication of the Martin Marty Center at the University of Chicago Divinity School. g":"questioning","taxonomy":"post_tag","url":"https://api.onbeing.org/tag/questioning/","path":"/tag/questioning/","termId":572,"termGroup":0,"termTaxonomyId":572,"description":"","parent":0,"count":7,"filter":"raw"},{"id":295,"name":"religion","slug":"religion","taxonomy":"post_tag","url":"https://api.onbeing.org/tag/religion/","path":"/tag/religion/","termId":295,"termGroup":0,"termTaxonomyId":295,"description":"","parent":0,"count":113,"filter":"raw"}],"content":"\<p>On the first Sunday in May, at a Mormon church in Utah, a 12-year-old girl \<a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=U89LnISqd8k\">came out to her congregation as a lesbian\</a>. What must have been one of the most meaningful events in young Savannah’s life was, with her permission, captured in a video by a friend who had come to lend support. This video not only documents the bravery of a queer girl in a conservative faith community, but the inevitable pushback by that community. Two-and-a-half minutes into her testimony, the ecclesiastical leader directing the meeting turned off the microphone and asked her to sit down. Savannah, apparently, had been silenced.\</p>\n\<p>Not for long.\</p>\n\<p>The video went viral and predictable outrage ensued. First \<a href=\"https://www.facebook.com/mamadragons/photos/a.1042491325849268.1073741829.904988119599590/1361653340599730/?type=3&amp;theater\">progressive Mormons\</a> — myself included — then the LGBTQ world at large were appalled, and understandably so. The video seems to tell it all: Powerful white males in suits and ties shut down the voice of a little girl who is speaking from the heart. News organizations from all over jumped on the story. Savannah, to her credit, managed amidst the media feeding frenzy to be \<a href=\"https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=-tEBUOXm5-A\">an articulate advocate for herself and other LGBTQ people\</a>. She described how she felt as she returned to her seat at church that day:\</p>\n\<blockquote>\<p>\<em>“I started crying because I wanted to finish my message&#8230;but I felt happy that people could hear [some] of it&#8230;and that I was being there for someone that probably needed it.”\</em>\</p>\</blockquote>\n\<p>But despite the sincerity of Savannah’s gesture, as the video was tweeted and posted and Instagrammed over and over, I reevaluated my attitude toward the man who cut the mic. I was troubled by how this story was playing out. The complexity that the events in this testimony meeting represented were getting lost. People were lining up on one side or another. Conservative Mormons were suspect of Savannah’s motives. They were sure her ex-Mormon mother had put her up to it, and grateful to the leaders for taking a stand.\</p>\n\<p>Progressive Mormons felt betrayed, wondering why the innocent words of a 12-year-old girl could threaten grown men. Outside the church, more and more well-meaning LGBTQ advocates seemed to jump onto the Savannah train in order to prove their devotion to the cause.\</p>\n\<p>I am a lifelong Mormon. My ancestors were pioneers who made the iconic trek across the plains to escape persecution. My Church has been a deeply nourishing source of spirit and inspiration for me, often an opportunity to do the hard work of serving in a Christian community where my attitudes and perspectives are very different from my fellow congregants. For a long time I was resigned to there being no place for gay members; their story didn’t fit into the LDS heteronormative model of family on earth and in heaven. I saw friends leave. I wished them well. I heard of parents shunning their gay children, of suicides. I turned away.\</p>\n\<p>Then, gradually, I started to question this divide. Did gay and trans people have to leave? How could I use the spiritual principles I had been practicing all these years to make the church a safe and welcoming place for all my brothers and sisters?\</p>\n\<figure id=\"attachment_35006\" style=\"max-width: 3912px\" class=\"wp-caption alignnone\">\<img data-attachment-id=\"35006\" data-permalink=\"https://onbeing.org/blog/erika-munson-the-challenge-of-not-choosing-sides/activists-protest-mormon-churchs-ban-on-baptizing-gay-children/\" data-orig-file=\"https://i2.wp.com/api.onbeing.org/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/GettyImages-496459110.jpg?fit=3912%2C2992&amp;ssl=1\" data-orig-size=\"3912,2992\" data-comments-opened=\"1\" data-image-meta=\"{&quot;aperture&quot;:&quot;3.5&quot;,&quot;credit&quot;:&quot;Getty Images&quot;,&quot;camera&quot;:&quot;Canon EOS-1D X&quot;,&quot;caption&quot;:&quot;SALT LAKE CITY, UT - NOVEMBER 9: Candice Green Barret (R) shows her daughter Quinn a balloon at a rally and party in support of gay and lesbian families sponsored by the Utah Pride Center on November 9, 2015 in Salt Lake City, Utah. Last week the Mormon Church announced changes to their policies to  classify people who enter into gay and lesbian marriages as apostates and ban their children from being blessed and baptized into the Mormon Church. Barret&#039;s daughter Quinn  will not be able to be baptized into the Mormon Church because her lesbian mother married. (Photo by George Frey\\/Getty Images&quot;,&quot;created_timestamp&quot;:&quot;1447119600&quot;,&quot;copyright&quot;:&quot;2015 Getty Images&quot;,&quot;focal_length&quot;:&quot;200&quot;,&quot;iso&quot;:&quot;3200&quot;,&quot;shutter_speed&quot;:&quot;0.016666666666667&quot;,&quot;title&quot;:&quot;Activists Protest Mormon Church&#039;s Ban On Baptizing Gay Children&quot;,&quot;orientation&quot;:&quot;0&quot;}\" data-image-title=\"Activists Protest Mormon Church&#8217;s Ban On Baptizing Gay Children\" data-image-description=\"\" data-medium-file=\"https://i2.wp.com/api.onbeing.org/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/GettyImages-496459110.jpg?fit=300%2C229&amp;ssl=1\" data-large-file=\"https://i2.wp.com/api.onbeing.org/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/GettyImages-496459110.jpg?fit=1200%2C918&amp;ssl=1\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-35006\" src=\"https://i2.wp.com/api.onbeing.org/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/GettyImages-496459110.jpg?resize=3912%2C2992&#038;ssl=1\" alt=\"Candice Green Barret shows her baby daughter Quinn a balloon at a rally and party in support of gay and lesbian families in Salt Lake City, Utah. Church doctrine forbids Quinn from being baptized into the LDS Church because her mother is lesbian and married. (Photo by George Frey/Getty Images)\" width=\"3912\" height=\"2992\" srcset=\"https://i2.wp.com/api.onbeing.org/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/GettyImages-496459110.jpg?w=3912&amp;ssl=1 3912w, https://i2.wp.com/api.onbeing.org/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/GettyImages-496459110.jpg?resize=300%2C229&amp;ssl=1 300w, https://i2.wp.com/api.onbeing.org/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/GettyImages-496459110.jpg?resize=768%2C587&amp;ssl=1 768w, https://i2.wp.com/api.onbeing.org/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/GettyImages-496459110.jpg?resize=1200%2C918&amp;ssl=1 1200w, https://i2.wp.com/api.onbeing.org/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/GettyImages-496459110.jpg?w=2000&amp;ssl=1 2000w, https://i2.wp.com/api.onbeing.org/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/GettyImages-496459110.jpg?w=3000&amp;ssl=1 3000w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 1000px) 100vw, 1000px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" />\<figcaption class=\"wp-caption-text\">\<i>Candice Green Barret shows her baby daughter Quinn a balloon at a rally and party in support of gay and lesbian families in Salt Lake City, Utah. Church doctrine forbids Quinn from being baptized into the LDS Church because her mother is lesbian and married.\</i>\<i>\<span class=\"caption-customfield\"> (\<a href=\"http://www.gettyimages.com/license/496459110\">George Frey\</a> / Getty Images / © All Rights Reserved) \</span>\</i>\</figcaption>\</figure>\n\<p>I was not alone in this impulse. In the last six years we have seen subtle but substantive changes in attitudes from members and leadership alike. To the outside world, Mormons may still seem stuck in the 1950s but the fact that it even occurred to Savannah to stand and declare her orientation in a church meeting speaks to a new willingness that Latter-day Saints have to \<a href=\"https://onbeing.org/programs/joanna-brooks-mormons-demystified/\">grapple with issues of faith and identity\</a>.\</p>\n\<p>But change in attitude does not mean change in doctrine. The LDS Church still considers homosexual relationships sinful. Heterosexual marriage is the only way into Mormon heaven. Our male lay clergy (and, yeah, that’s a whole other story) are called to enforce the definition of same-sex bonds as “apostate.” When they accept a position in local leadership, this is one of the tenets they are agreeing to uphold.\</p>\n\<p>As LGBTQ advocates from all quarters proclaimed Savannah a rainbow hero, I found myself dropping some of my righteous indignation and seeking compassion for the men in charge of that meeting. What were they experiencing when Savannah talked of her conviction that God does not want her to be alone and her hopes someday of marriage to a woman? How were they supposed to toe the line of orthodoxy and stand as witnesses of Christ&#8217;s unconditional love?\</p>\n\<p>This is the essential question that Latter-day Saints face in issues of sexual orientation and gender identity. For some Mormons the answer is clear — health and wholeness require them to leave the Church. I honor this path. But for others like myself, \<a href=\"https://onbeing.org/programs/joy-ladin-transgender-amid-orthodoxy-i-am-who-i-will-be-mar2017/\">living in the tension of orthodoxy and inclusion is rich with meaning\</a>.\</p>\n\<p>It demands empathy and patience, attention to inner wisdom. It asks you not to take sides in internet battles, not to shame and dehumanize an entire cohort of humanity in order to show support for your own idea of what someone or something represents.\</p>\n\<p>In a Mormon testimony meeting you will see members rise when moved by the Spirit, and speak of what they know to be true. Often it’s a rote catechism: Joseph Smith was a prophet, the Book of Mormon is God’s word. It’s not unusual to hear personal stories of what Jesus’ atonement means in someone’s life. And there’s always the chance of a memorable but incomprehensible narrative that in the telling helps the speaker feel less alone.\</p>\n\<figure id=\"attachment_35008\" style=\"max-width: 4763px\" class=\"wp-caption alignnone\">\<img data-attachment-id=\"35008\" data-permalink=\"https://onbeing.org/blog/erika-munson-the-challenge-of-not-choosing-sides/the-church-of-jesus-christ-of-latter-day-saints-holds-news-conference-on-religious-freedom-and-nondiscrimination/\" data-orig-file=\"https://i1.wp.com/api.onbeing.org/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/GettyImages-462356094.jpg?fit=4763%2C3081&amp;ssl=1\" data-orig-size=\"4763,3081\" data-comments-opened=\"1\" data-image-meta=\"{&quot;aperture&quot;:&quot;2.8&quot;,&quot;credit&quot;:&quot;Getty Images&quot;,&quot;camera&quot;:&quot;Canon EOS-1D X&quot;,&quot;caption&quot;:&quot;SALT LAKE CITY, UT - JANUARY 27: (L-R) Mormon Apostle Dallin Oaks, Mormon Young Women&#039;s Presidency member, Neill Marriott, and Mormon Apostle Todd Christofferson announce at a news conference The Church of Jesus Christ Of Latter-Day Saints stance on religious freedom and nondiscrimination when it comes to gay and LGBT issues currently being litigated in the United States and around the world on January 27, 2014 in Salt Lake City, Utah. The church announced it would seek legislation to protect the rights of those in the LGBT commutinty as long as religoius freedom weas also addressed.  (Photo by George Frey\\/Getty Images&quot;,&quot;created_timestamp&quot;:&quot;1396718040&quot;,&quot;copyright&quot;:&quot;2015 Getty Images&quot;,&quot;focal_length&quot;:&quot;70&quot;,&quot;iso&quot;:&quot;1250&quot;,&quot;shutter_speed&quot;:&quot;0.00625&quot;,&quot;title&quot;:&quot;The Church Of Jesus Christ of Latter-Day Saints Holds News Conference On Religious Freedom And Nondiscrimination&quot;,&quot;orientation&quot;:&quot;0&quot;}\" data-image-title=\"The Church Of Jesus Christ of Latter-Day Saints Holds News Conference On Religious Freedom And Nondiscrimination\" data-image-description=\"\" data-medium-file=\"https://i1.wp.com/api.onbeing.org/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/GettyImages-462356094.jpg?fit=300%2C194&amp;ssl=1\" data-large-file=\"https://i1.wp.com/api.onbeing.org/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/GettyImages-462356094.jpg?fit=1200%2C776&amp;ssl=1\" class=\"aligncenter size-full wp-image-35008\" src=\"https://i1.wp.com/api.onbeing.org/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/GettyImages-462356094.jpg?resize=4763%2C3081&#038;ssl=1\" alt=\"Mormon Apostle Dallin Oaks, Mormon Young Women's Presidency member, Neill Marriott, and Mormon Apostle Todd Christofferson announce at a news conference The Church of Jesus Christ Of Latter-day Saints stance on religious freedom and nondiscrimination when it comes to gay and LGBT issues being litigated in the United States and around the world on January 27, 2014 in Salt Lake City, Utah. The church announced it would seek legislation to protect the rights of those in the LGBT community as long as religious freedom was also addressed.\" width=\"4763\" height=\"3081\" srcset=\"https://i1.wp.com/api.onbeing.org/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/GettyImages-462356094.jpg?w=4763&amp;ssl=1 4763w, https://i1.wp.com/api.onbeing.org/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/GettyImages-462356094.jpg?resize=300%2C194&amp;ssl=1 300w, https://i1.wp.com/api.onbeing.org/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/GettyImages-462356094.jpg?resize=768%2C497&amp;ssl=1 768w, https://i1.wp.com/api.onbeing.org/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/GettyImages-462356094.jpg?resize=1200%2C776&amp;ssl=1 1200w, https://i1.wp.com/api.onbeing.org/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/GettyImages-462356094.jpg?w=2000&amp;ssl=1 2000w, https://i1.wp.com/api.onbeing.org/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/GettyImages-462356094.jpg?w=3000&amp;ssl=1 3000w\" sizes=\"(max-width: 1000px) 100vw, 1000px\" data-recalc-dims=\"1\" />\<figcaption class=\"wp-caption-text\">\<i>Mormon Apostle Dallin Oaks, Mormon Young Women&#8217;s Presidency member, Neill Marriott, and Mormon Apostle Todd Christofferson announce at a news conference The Church of Jesus Christ Of Latter-day Saints stance on religious freedom and nondiscrimination when it comes to gay and LGBT issues being litigated in the United States and around the world on January 27, 2014 in Salt Lake City, Utah. The church announced it would seek legislation to protect the rights of those in the LGBT community as long as religious freedom was also addressed.\</i>\<i>\<span class=\"caption-customfield\"> (\<a href=\"http://www.gettyimages.com/license/462356094\">George Frey\</a> / Getty Images / © All Rights Reserved) \</span>\</i>\</figcaption>\</figure>\n\<p>There’s a truth I can testify to out there that’s even bigger than a 12-year-old’s courage or a clergyman’s duty to his beloved church. That truth lies in the answer to this question: Both Savannah and her leaders are inherently worthy of love and belonging in our community, so \<a href=\"https://onbeing.org/blog/burton-visotzky-joseph-jarvis-what-unites-us-is-greater/\">how do we make that love, that belonging, a reality\</a>? And since the answer is so far from our grasp at the moment, what does just “living in the question,” as Rilke puts it, look like?\</p>\n\<p>My hunch is that it has something to do with the spirit we bring to the week-to-week activities of a congregation: the scripture study, the Sunday worship, the service projects, the church suppers. Can I let the gay and trans kids know I am their ally? Can I listen to their parents? Likewise, can I forge relationships of understanding and respect with my ecclesiastical leaders: my bishop, Steve, who loves his motorcycle; his counselor, Rob, who is raising his blended family of eight with joy? Can I look beyond their suits and ties, their power to silence, and see them as individuals who struggle just like I do?\</p>\n\<p>If I’m going to continue my journey in the conservative faith community I was born into, the answer has to be yes. And until humanity finds a way to wrestle the internet into submission, when the next faith controversy hits social media (tomorrow? the next day?) I’ll \<a href=\"https://onbeing.org/blog/shine-a-light-on-the-good-and-beautiful/\">resist the temptation to weaponize it\</a>.\</p>\n","excerpt":"A young, gay Mormon’s testimony sparked a rift in her community — but, Erika Munson wonders, must we give in to the instinct to take sides? On lingering in the complex questions with a spirit of compassion that has room for our differences.","url":"https://onbeing.org/blog/erika-munson-the-challenge-of-not-choosing-sides/","path":"/blog/erika-munson-the-challenge-of-not-choosing-sides/","metadata":{"type":"post","featuredMedia":"https://i2.wp.com/api.onbeing.org/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/GettyImages-130218146.jpg?fit=3600%2C2396&ssl=1","imageCredit":"","imageCreditLink":"","episode":{"title":"","audioUrl":"","audioUrlUnedited":"","audioUrlUneditedFile":"","audioUrlEmbed":"","description":"","shortDescription":"","guests":[]}},"episodeMeta":{"releaseDate":"","title":"","strapline":"","shortDescription":"","description":"","guests":"","audioUrl":""},"type":"post","featuredMedia":"https://i2.wp.com/api.onbeing.org/wp-content/uploads/2017/09/GettyImages-130218146.jpg?fit=3600%2C2396&ssl=1"},{"id":28685,"date":"2017-05-16T16:53:13+00:00","modifiedDate":"2017-05-16T16:53:13+00:00","slug":"cheryl-strayed-the-power-of-me-too","title":"The Power of \"Me Too\"","author":{"id":"992","slug":"cheryl-strayed","description":"\<p>is the New York Times bestselling author of \<em>\<a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/gp/product/0307476073\">Wild\</a>\</em>, \<em>\<a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Tiny-Beautiful-Things-Advice-Sugar/dp/0307949338/\">Tiny Beautiful Things\</a>\</em>, \<em>\<a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Brave-Enough-Cheryl-Strayed/dp/1101946903/\">Brave Enough\</a>\</em>, and \<em>\<a href=\"https://www.amazon.com/Torch-Vintage-Contemporaries-Cheryl-Strayed/dp/0345805615/\">Torch\</a>\</em>. Her essays have been published in \<em>The Best American Essays\</em>, \<em>The New York Times\</em>, \<em>The Washington Post Magazine\</em>, \<em>Vogue\</em>, \<em>Salon\</em>, \<em>The Sun\</em>, \<em>Tin House\</em>, \<em>The New York Times Book Review\</em> and elsewhere. Strayed is the co-host, along with Steve Almond, of WBUR’s hit podcast \<a href=\"http://www.wbur.org/dearsugar\">Dear Sugar Radio\</a>, which originated with her popular “Dear Sugar” advice column on \<em>The Rumpus\</em>.\</p>\n","guestDescription":"","name":"Cheryl
